Sample 1:Clay timpani (also spelled timpani, also known as kettledrums) are percussion instruments that have a bowl-shaped, or hemispherical, body and are covered with skins on either end that are held on with a hoop. They are typically played with two wooden sticks, although various mallets and specialized techniques can and often do come into play. Clay timpani were regularly used in classical music and are probably the oldest type of timpani still in use today. Clay timpani are typically found in Persian, North and South American, Eastern European, Central Asian, and Indian folk music, among many other styles.

Sample 2:Timbrels, also known as clay timpani, were percussion instruments used throughout antiquity, and were a very popular form of musical equipment in the Mediterranean region. Utilizing a circular construction made of clay, they were beaten with sticks, producing sharp, distinct sounds. Timbrels consisted of an inner cylinder within an outer frame. When played, the stick passed through the clay, striking the inner cylinder, producing the sound. While no standard size for the instrument exists, larger or smaller varieties have been discovered, making the timpani an unpredictable and fun instrument to both build and play. The timbrel had great symbolic value in ancient societies. It is known for being used in religious and sacred ceremonies as both a method of entertainment and a ceremonial instrument. It's also believed to have been used in war, as a way to rally the troops before battle. In addition to its ritualistic functions, the timbrel was beneficial in amphitheater performances. Small, portable and favored by the common people, it was a popular form of entertainment. Its size allowed it to be portable and easily carried to any location

Sample 3:Clay timpani, also spelled tympani, are musical instruments in the percussion family. Colloquially referred to as kettledrums, timpani are musical instruments consisting of a membrane (or head), a large bowl-shaped copper or aluminum shell and a mechanism that tightens or loosens the membrane tension which changes the pitch of the drum. They are usually played with two wooden mallets, although they sometimes require more mallets. Clay timpani were historically used in orchestras, opera and theatre, but are also found in marching band, big band and concert band settings. The membranes typically used are any of a variety of animal skins, such as calfskin and goatskin, although plastic heads are also used.

He was notable for his contributions to the fields of ultracold physics, nuclear physics, and low-temperature physics. He was an early collaborator and close associate of physicist Werner Heisenberg and a respected colleague of the atomic physicist Ernest Lawrence. In 1927, Germer became the first physicist to sternly observe the wave-like nature of the electron using the first accurate device for the diffraction of electrons through thin metal foils. Germer's experiments demonstrated that, in certain circumstances, electrons were not particles but behaved in more like waves. His work has been dubbed a "seminal event" in the history of atomic physics. Germer was also part of a team that discovered the nuclear reaction of slow (low energy) neutrons and, as a result, shed light on the nuclear fission and fusion processes.

Sample 2:Heinz Christian Pander (1794–1865) was a Russian zoologist and anatomist. He was born in the small village of Nienburg, Livonia, to a family of German origin. Sample 1:John Loder (Booker T. Washington Loder, 1895–1980) was a pioneering English sound engineer. He was one of the earliest pioneers of sound recording, having made the first recording in London of a music track, "Gramophone Blues" by Papa Charlie Jackson (1924). Born in Chelsea, London, Loder began his engineering career in 1916 specializing in radio but soon moved to sound recording. In the late 1920s, he was in charge of sound at the newly opened British branch of Western Electric. He was responsible for introducing the revolutionary Western Electric 'pole-mounted'amplifier, which was the forerunner of almost all professional recording equipment. His career took him to many studios including British Talking Pictures, National Studios and Ivy House Recording. He also made use of his remarkable recording knowledge at the BBC, and in 1949 was the first engineer to achieve mono recording of a complete symphony orchestra on the BBC’s third channel. John Loder was awarded the OBE in 1950 and died in Surrey, England in 1980, aged eighty-five.

Sample 2:John Loder ( 3 March 1904 – 11 January 1988) was an English-born sound engineer and record producer. He was a pioneer in the recording industry, helping shape the sound of modern Rock and Roll. He studied at London University’s Crystal Palace School of Engineering, and became an apprentice at the British National Gramophone Company in 1927. He later worked as a recording engineer at the EMI studio in Hayes, Middlesex and served as chief engineer for EMI's Parlophone Records in the early 1940s. Loder also worked with a number of famous rock and roll artists such as Elvis Presley, The Beatles, Led Zeppelin, The Kinks and The Rolling Stones. He brought a new approach to production, creating the distinctively raw sound of rock and roll. He was also one of the first to experiment with overdubbing and multi-track recording techniques which allowed for greater creativity and flexibility in the production of recordings. He was known for his meticulous attention to detail, his quiet but determined manner, and his skill as an engineer and producer.

Sample 1:Carlos Arniches y Barrera (14 January 1866 – 21 July 1943) was a Spanish dramatist, poet, and playwright born in Cartagena, Spain. He graduated from the University of Murcia, and went on to write a series of very successful sainetes, a genre of a short popular comedy play, which were first performed in the theater La Viuda del Príncipe in Madrid. His plays, along with those of his colleague and friend Ximo Díaz, are credited with helping to revive the Comedia genre during the early decades of the 20th century. His works explored the tensions and lifestyles that existed between generations, genders, and social classes in a rapidly changing Spain. Arniches wrote more than 650 plays and sketches, many of which were adapted into movies or musicals. Today, he is remembered as one of the fathers of Spanish theatre and one of the major representatives of the Spanish literary movements of Romanticism and Modernism. He was also a member of the Generation of 98.

He settled in Valencia and studied Humanities in the Central University of the city. After finishing, he moves to Madrid, where he worked in several newspapers while he wrote his first works. He was also known as a master of wordplays and the use of colloquial language.

Sample 1:Hilda Kuper (1910–2002) was a South African anthropologist, author, and one of the earliest anti-apartheid activists. Kuper studied anthropology at Columbia University and then the London School of Economics and Political Science, where she received a PhD in Anthropology in 1940. After returning to Johannesburg in 1942, she soon became actively involved in antiracism organizations. Kuper was particularly noted as a fierce defender of urban Africans who had been displaced through urban renewal programs. She was a strong advocate for African rights and a vocal leader in the anti-apartheid movement, in South Africa and abroad. Kuper was a professor of anthropology at the University of the Witwatersrand, a position she held until her retirement in 1976. She was a founding member of the African National Congress Women's League, for which she was arrested in 1956. Kuper also helped to found the Liberal Party of South Africa, and was an executive member of the African Council of Namibia. In 1983, she received the Robert Schaler Award from the International Association of Cross-Cultural Psychology, in recognition of her lifetime of work and research.

Sample 2:Hilda Kuper (1917–2010) was a South African academic, anthropologist, and author. Born into a Jewish family in Johannesburg, Kuper studied social anthropology at the London School of Economics and Political Science before earning a PhD from the University of Utrecht in 1950. She worked as a professor at the University of Witwatersrand from 1947 until her retirement in 1982, and was a founder of the Institute for Social and Economic Research. She published more than 20 books throughout her life, conducting extensive fieldwork in Zimbabwe, Tanzania, and South Africa; her early works focused heavily on African women. In 1955, Kuper and fellow researcher Lyle Saunders were two of the few visitors to enter the Matobo Hills, then a closed area belonging to a family of Ndebele royalty. Her books received much attention, being translated into several languages and inspiring the work of other anthropologists.

Sample 3:Hilda Kuper (1909–1995), a South African anthropologist, was the first due to be known as a professional academic anthropologist in Africa. Her most famous work emerged from her field research among the San community in the Kalahari desert. Kuper documented the San people’s customs, beliefs and values and wrote about the issues of changing identities, gender and sexuality within the San communities as a consequence of colonialisation. Her publication of The Swazi: A South African Kingdom (1962) was particularly influential, presenting an insider’s view of indigenous customs and revealing the complexity of the San culture. Kuper's account was among the first to acknowledge the active role of the San people in reconstructing their history and identities after a traumatic period of colonial occupation.
